Refutation of the Mu'tazili View and Proof of the Enduring's Strength
Author refutes Mu'tazili view on annihilation and argues enduring is stronger in existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazili claim that accidents cease by the incidence of a contrary, and the argument that the enduring (al-baqi) is stronger in existence than the newly occurring (al-hadith).

Core Idea
A pre-existing contrary is no more deserving of non-existence than a newly arriving one.

Having classified the various views on annihilation, the author now focuses on the Mu'tazilite position that an accident ceases when its contrary attacks it. He argues that this is impossible because the pre-existing contrary is not more worthy of non-existence than the newly arriving contrary; the choice is arbitrary. He then provides a further proof that the state of being enduring (baqi) is stronger in existence than being newly occurring (hadith), by comparing necessity and contingency of existence, and by noting that the enduring has prior existence which gives it strength.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the Mu'tazili view
The author uses the principle that if two contraries exist, the pre-existing one is no more entitled to remain than the intruder. Thus, the Mu'tazili explanation is arbitrary.
This refutes the idea that a newly occurring contrary can actively annihilate its counterpart.
2
Examine the argument about strength in existence
Notice how the author contrasts the enduring (al-baqi) with the newly occurring (al-hadith): the enduring's existence does not depend on a power that can be withheld, while the newly occurring does. Also, the enduring is necessary of existence in that state, whereas the newly occurring is contingent.
This is a separate positive argument to support the Maturidi position.
3
Understand the role of prior existence
The author points out that the enduring had existence before the incidence of the contrary, which gives it strength. The newly occurring had no prior existence, so its non-existence does not indicate weakness.
This counters any claim that the newly occurring is stronger because it just came into being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(2) Cutting off endurance—held by Dirar, Ash'ari, and Ka'bi, but this requires that endurance be created in bodies, not in accidents. (3) Cutting off colors and modes—held by al-Baqillani, only applicable to bodies. (4) Incidence of a contrary—the Mu'tazilite view. The author refutes (4) by arguing that if a pre-existing contrary is stable, it is no more deserving of non-existence than the newly arriving contrary; thus the Mu'tazilite argument is arbitrary. Then he provides a further proof that the state of being enduring is stronger in existence than being newly occurring. The enduring's existence is not dependent on a power that can be withheld, while the newly occurring's existence is contingent on power. Also, the enduring is necessary (wajib) in that state, whereas the newly occurring is contingent (ja'iz). The author also points out that the enduring had existence before the state of the striking contrary, which gives it strength, while the newly occurring had no prior existence. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about the newly occurring accident.

Key Terms
باقي (baqi: enduring)
حادث (hadith: newly occurring)
ضد (didd: contrary)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud: necessary of existence)
جائز الوجود (ja'iz al-wujud: contingent of existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The newly occurring (hadith) is stronger in existence than the enduring (baqi).
Correction: The author argues this is arbitrary; in fact, the enduring is stronger because its existence is necessary and not dependent on power.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what evidence shows that the enduring is stronger in existence than the newly occurring?
A) The enduring is contingent, the newly occurring is necessary.
B) The enduring's existence depends on God's power.
C) The enduring is necessary of existence and has prior existence.
D) The newly occurring has prior existence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view of annihilation by a contrary is arbitrary, and the enduring is stronger in existence due to necessity and prior existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily struggles, do you habitually rely on things that are newly occurring and contingent (like your own efforts, others' approval, or material possessions) rather than on the Enduring, Necessary Being? What is one specific area where you need to shift your reliance today, and how will you remind yourself that the enduring is stronger?
?
When a difficult situation (a 'contrary') enters your life, do you feel as if the good that was there is automatically nullified? How does this lesson's argument—that the pre-existing is not more deserving of non-existence than the new—challenge you to hold onto faith in God's enduring goodness despite trials?
?
If you truly accepted that your own existence is contingent (ja'iz al-wujud) and that only Allah is necessary (wajib al-wujud), what would change about your sense of security and your priorities? Commit to one concrete act of trust today that reflects this truth.

Objection on Endurance and Divine Power Answered
Response to objection that an enduring body is not an object of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response defends that divine power directly pertains to accidents (like endurance) rather than the enduring substance itself.

Core Idea
Divine power acts on accidents, not on enduring substances directly.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if a body endures through an accident, then the body itself cannot be an object of divine power. The author responds by clarifying that power never directly applies to an existing substance but only to the accidents that inhere in it. The argument uses an analogy with motion to show consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opponent's claim and the author's reply.
The objection assumes power applies directly to the body.
2
Identify the opponent's assumption
The opponent thinks that if endurance is an accident, then the body is not directly an object of power. The author refutes this by showing that even without endurance, the body is not an object of power.
This reveals a deeper principle: power does not pertain to existent substances.
3
Trace the author's counter-argument
First, the author negates the opponent's premise about endurance. Then he adds that power does not pertain to the existent at all. Finally, he uses the analogy of motion to show that power creates the accident, not the substance.
The analogy is key: just as power to move creates motion, power to preserve creates endurance.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for the doctrine of divine attributes. It preserves that God's power is effective on accidents while the substance remains a locus.
This is a classic Maturidi position distinguishing substance from accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما سألوا عنه قولهم: إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا يقوم به لم يكن نفسه مقدورا للقديم (باقيًا؟ والجواب عنه أن نفي البقاء لم يجعل النفس مقدوراً للقديم) بوصف البقاء أيضا، ونزيد عليه بأن نقول : لا يكون مقدورًا له في حال الوجود، لأن القدرة عندهم لا تتعلق بالموجود. ثم يقال لهذا السائل: لو كان المتحرك متحركاً لمعنى لم يكن الجسم مقدورًا للقديم متحركا ؟ فإن قال : القدرة على تحريكه قدرة على خلق الحركة له. قيل له: والقدرة على إبقائه، قدرة على خلق بقائه.
And among what they asked is their saying: If the body were enduring by an attribute subsisting in it, then it would not itself be an object of power (maqdur) for the Eternal (as enduring?). The answer is that negating endurance did not make the self an object of power for the Eternal with the attribute of endurance either. And we add to it by saying: It is not an object of power for Him in the state of existence, because power, according to them, does not pertain to the existent. Then it is said to this questioner: If the mover were moving by an attribute, would the body not be an object of power for the Eternal as moving? If he says: The power to move it is a power to create motion for it. It is said to him: And the power to preserve it (ibqa') is a power to create its endurance (baqa').
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and its answer. The objector argues that if a body endures by an accident (i.e., a created attribute subsisting in it), then the body itself would not be an object of divine power (maqdur) with respect to its endurance. The author answers that negating endurance does not make the body an object of power with the attribute of endurance either. Moreover, they add that the body is not an object of power in the state of existence at all, because power (qudra) according to the opponents (the Mu'tazila or Ash'ari?) does not pertain to the existent once it exists. Then the author turns the argument: if a moving object moves by an attribute (i.e., an accident of motion), then would the body not be an object of power as moving? The opponent would likely respond that the power to move it is actually the power to create motion in it. The author then analogizes: similarly, the power to preserve (ibqa') the body is the power to create endurance in it. Thus, the objection fails because the body is not directly the object of power; rather, the accident is.

Key Terms
مقدور
قدرة
إبقاء
تحريك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine power directly controls the continued existence of a substance itself.
Correction: The passage shows that power creates the accident of endurance, not the substance. The substance is already existent and not directly an object of power.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, the power to preserve a body is power to create what?
A) The body itself
B) The endurance of the body
C) A new substance
D) The soul

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi kalam, divine power directly creates accidents, not the substances that bear them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly feel that your very existence each moment is a direct act of God's power creating accidents, or do you take your continued being for granted as a fixed state? What would change in your daily life if you remembered that your preservation is a continuous act of creation?

If divine power does not directly act on your substance but only on accidents, then your 'self' is not a static object but a series of momentary experiences sustained by God. Does this thought humble you or unsettle you, and why?

The Maturidi Position on the Endurance of God's Attributes
Discussion of whether God's attributes endure, presenting the Maturidi view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi view on whether God's attributes (like knowledge) are described as enduring, and how it differs from the Ash'ari and early Maturidi positions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge endures by itself, not by a separate attribute of endurance.

This passage addresses a key question in Islamic theology: are God's attributes themselves enduring? The author presents three distinct positions: the early Maturidis, al-Ash'ari, and the later Maturidi verifiers. Understanding this distinction clarifies how attributes relate to God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the objection at the beginning and the three-part answer.
2
Identify the three positions
The answer lists three views: early Maturidis, Ash'ari, and Maturidi verifiers. Underline each part in the text.
3
Understand the Maturidi verifiers' nuanced view
Focus on the phrase 'بقاء لنفسه' — enduring by itself. This is the key point: knowledge does not need a separate attribute of endurance.
Compare with the other views to see the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : إنكم تقولون : إن علم الله تعالى باق وكذا سائر صفاته، وقيام صفة البقاء بها مستحيل؟ والجواب: إنا بينا أن علم الله تعالى عند قدماء أصحابنا غير موصوف بالبقاء فلم يتوجه عليهم الإلزام، وعند الأشعري كان الله باقيًا بصفاته فكان بقاؤه بقاء لصفاته. والمحققون من أصحابنا يقولون : إن علمه تعالى للذات بقاء لنفسه فلم يكن باقيًا بدون البقاء، وقد استقصينا الكلام فيه وتقصينا عن عهدة ما يتوجه عليه من الإشكال في مسألة الصفات، والله الموفق.
Then he said: You say that the knowledge of God Most High is enduring (baqi) and likewise the rest of His attributes, and the subsistence of the attribute of endurance in them is impossible? The answer: We have made clear that the knowledge of God Most High, according to the early (qudama') of our companions, is not described with endurance (baqa'), so the objection does not apply to them. And according to al-Ash'ari, God was enduring by His attributes, so His endurance was an endurance for His attributes. And the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among our companions say: His knowledge, Most High, is for the essence an endurance by itself, so it was not enduring without endurance. And we have exhausted the discussion on it and discharged ourselves from the responsibility of the difficulty that arises concerning the question of attributes, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses a question about whether God's attributes (like knowledge) are described as enduring (baqi). The questioner points out that the Maturidis say God's knowledge is enduring, but then the attribute of endurance cannot subsist in another attribute (since attributes do not inhere in attributes). The answer distinguishes positions: According to the early Maturidi scholars (qudama'), God's knowledge is not described with endurance at all, so the objection does not apply. According to al-Ash'ari, God is enduring by His attributes, so His endurance applies to His attributes as well (i.e., the attributes share in His endurance). The verifiers among the Maturidis hold a middle view: God's knowledge endures by itself (li al-dhat baqa' li nafsihi), meaning it does not require a separate attribute of endurance; it is inherently enduring. Thus, there is no need for an attribute of endurance to subsist in knowledge. The author claims to have fully addressed this and resolved any difficulties.

Key Terms

صفات
بقاء
ذات
أشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Maturidi view requires an additional attribute of endurance for God's attributes.
Correction: The Maturidi verifiers specifically state that knowledge is enduring by itself (li nafsihi), so no separate attribute of endurance is needed.

Checkpoint

B) By the endurance of God
C) By itself (li nafsihi)
D) It is not enduring

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi verifiers hold that God's knowledge is inherently enduring, not by a separate attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it look like to live as if that were true?
?
The early scholars did not even describe God's knowledge with 'endurance'—they were so careful about attributing anything to God beyond what He revealed. Is your speech about God as cautious? When was the last time you caught yourself saying something about Allah without evidence, and what will you do to prevent that from happening again?

Addressing the Objection to the Endurance of God's Attributes
Al-Nasafi answers an objection about the endurance of God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi and Ash'ari scholars respond to the objection that God's attributes cannot be enduring because endurance itself would need an attribute.

Core Idea
God's knowledge endures without needing the attribute of endurance.

Al-Nasafi presents an objection against the eternity of God's attributes: if God's knowledge is enduring, then the attribute of endurance must subsist in it, which is impossible. He then gives three answers from different theological schools, showing the nuanced Maturidi perspective.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. The problem is: if knowledge is enduring, then endurance itself becomes an attribute subsisting in another attribute, which is impossible.
2
First answer: Early Maturidi
Identify the first answer: early Maturidis do not describe knowledge as enduring, so the objection fails.
3
Second answer: Ash'ari
Identify the second answer: according to al-Ash'ari, God is enduring with His attributes, so His endurance covers them.
4
Third answer: Maturidi verifiers
Identify the third answer: the verifying Maturidis hold that God's knowledge is an endurance for the Essence itself, so it does not require a separate attribute of endurance.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this discussion matters for the broader understanding of divine attributes and theological diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: إنكم تقولون: إن علم الله تعالى باق وكذا سائر صفاته، وقيام صفة البقاء بها مستحيل؟ والجواب: إنا بينا أن علم الله تعالى عند قدماء أصحابنا غير موصوف بالبقاء فلم يتوجه عليهم الإلزام، وعند الأشعري كان الله باقيًا بصفاته فكان بقاؤه بقاء لصفاته. والمحققون من أصحابنا يقولون: إن علمه تعالى للذات بقاء لنفسه فلم يكن باقيًا بدون البقاء، وقد استقصينا الكلام فيه وتقصينا عن عهدة ما يتوجه عليه من الإشكال في مسألة الصفات، والله الموفق.
If he says: You say that God's knowledge is enduring and likewise His other attributes, while the subsistence of the attribute of endurance in them is impossible? The answer: We have explained that God's knowledge, according to the early scholars among us, is not described by endurance, so the objection does not apply to them. And according to al-Ash'ari, God was enduring with His attributes, so His endurance was the endurance of His attributes. The verifiers among our scholars say: His knowledge is for the Essence an endurance for itself, so it was not enduring without endurance. And we have exhausted the discussion on it and discharged ourselves of any problem that may arise concerning the issue of the attributes. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the endurance (baqā') of God's attributes. The objector claims that if God's knowledge is eternal, then the attribute of endurance would subsist in it, but an attribute subsisting in another attribute is impossible. Al-Nasafi answers by distinguishing three positions: the early Maturidi scholars do not describe knowledge as 'enduring' at all, so the objection misses its mark. For al-Ash'ari, God Himself is enduring with His attributes, so His endurance covers them. The verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqūn) hold that God's knowledge is an endurance for the Essence itself, implying it persists without needing the attribute of endurance. Thus, the difficulty is resolved. Al-Nasafi notes that he has already given a thorough treatment elsewhere.

The Proof that Action is a Necessary Concomitant
Nasafi argues that action is a necessary concomitant of the Essence via conditional proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that action is a necessary concomitant of the divine essence.

Core Idea
Action is inseparable from the Essence; prolonged absence proves necessity.

This passage presents a concise logical argument. The author, Imam Nasafi, uses a conditional proof to establish that action (al-fi'l) is a necessary concomitant (muqtada) of the divine Essence. Understanding this reasoning is foundational for Maturidi theology regarding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional chain
Find the two conditional statements: (1) If act were not necessary, then absence for many times would be possible. (2) If absence for many times is impossible, then act is necessary.
The logic is a form of contrapositive.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Understand that 'necessary concomitant' means the Essence cannot exist without it. The proof shows that the impossibility of prolonged absence entails inseparability at all times.
This is a key concept in kalam regarding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقتضيات ذاتها إذ الذات لا تنفك عما تقتضيه، وإذا لم يكن الفعل من مقتضيات ذاتها جاز خلوها عنه أوقاتاً كثيرة، وإذا لم يجز خلوها عنه أوقاتا كثرة دل أنه من مقتضيات ذاتها فلا يتصور خلوها عنه وقتا.
The necessary concomitants of His Essence, because the Essence is not free from what it necessitates. And if the act were not among the necessary concomitants of His Essence, it would be possible for the Essence to be devoid of it for many times. And if it is not possible for the Essence to be devoid of it for many times, it indicates that it is among the necessary concomitants of His Essence, so its being devoid of it at any time is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that action (al-fi'l) is a necessary concomitant (muqtada) of the divine Essence (al-dhat). The argument begins by stating that the Essence is inseparable from what it necessitates. Then it uses a conditional reasoning: if the act were not a necessary concomitant, it would be possible for the Essence to be without it for many occasions. Conversely, if it is impossible for the Essence to be without the act for many occasions, that proves the act is a necessary concomitant, and thus the Essence cannot be devoid of it at any moment. The logic is that the possibility of prolonged absence implies non-necessity, while the impossibility of such absence implies necessity.

Key Terms
مقتضيات الذات
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary concomitant means temporal coexistence or that the act is a separate entity from the Essence.
Correction: It means logical inseparability: the Essence cannot exist without the act, not that they are separate in time or substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if the act is not a necessary concomitant of the Essence?
A) The act is impossible.
B) The Essence can be devoid of the act for many times.
C) The act is eternal.
D) The Essence cannot exist without the act.

Key Takeaway
Action is a necessary concomitant of the divine Essence, meaning the Essence cannot exist without it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If action is a necessary concomitant of God's Essence, then your existence is always dependent on His ongoing action. Does your life reflect that dependence? Where have you been acting as if you are autonomous?
?
The proof uses the impossibility of prolonged absence. Think of a time you felt God was absent. How does this lesson expose that feeling as a failure to understand His essence? Will you repent for that doubt and begin to see His presence in all things?

Divine Attributes

Refutation of Power for Another Act in the Second Moment
Nasafi refutes two attempts to separate power from its act in time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that divine power must directly accompany its act, and why separating them leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
Power must accompany its act or the act has no power.

Nasafi now addresses a nuanced objection: could an enduring power be 'reassigned' to a different act in its second moment? The opponent tries to avoid the conclusion that power must accompany its act by positing a chain of power-for-future-acts. But as you will see, this only pushes the problem back, never letting power and act coincide. Nasafi demonstrates that any such separation leads to an act without its proper power or to the impossible doctrine of accident continuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of argument.
Notice the two hypothetical opponent positions introduced by 'if they say...'.
2
Identify the opponent's first claim
The opponent claims that power in the state of continuance is for another act, not the one it accompanied initially. Note how Nasafi rephrases it.
This is the 'say of some of them'.
3
Follow Nasafi's first refutation
Nasafi says this is like the first objection. Why? Because if power is always for a future act, then no act ever has its own power — the act is established 'not by its power'. Understand why this is a problem.
4
Examine the opponent's second claim and its refutation
The opponent then suggests that in the second moment, power is for the act occurring in that same moment. Nasafi shows this defeats their own position: it means power does accompany its act, so all their efforts are void.
See how this leads to the collapse of their argument.
5
Reflect on the consequences
Nasafi concludes that the opponent's position leads to either the false doctrine of accident continuance (baqa' al-a'rad) or the absurdity of a power with no object. Appreciate how he exposes the internal inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدرة على هذا الفعل الذي اقترن بها وقت البقاء؟ أم كانت قدرة لا على هذا الفعل بل لفعل غيره يفعل بها في الثاني من وقت وجود هذا الفعل؟ فإن قالوا: هي في حالة البقاء قدرة على فعل آخر غير هذا الذي وجد في حال بقاءه وقدرة هذا الفعل هي فى أول أحوال وجودها ويتعقبها الفعل، ثم هي في الثاني في حال وجودها قدرة فعل آخر يوجد في الثالث من حال وجودها وهو قول بعضهم. قلنا هذا مثل الأول فإنها لما لم تكن فى كل حال قدرة لما يقارنها من الفعل بل هي قدرة على ما يتعقبه من الفعل كان كل فعل ثابتًا لا بما له من القدرة، ووجود ما هو قدرة على غيره لا يجدي نفعا ولا يدفع ما ألزمنا. وإن قالوا: هي في الثاني قدرة على ما ثبت فيه من الفعل المقارن فيها. قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الثاني قدرة على ما يثبت فيه من الفعل فكانت هي قدرة الفعل الذي اقترن بها، وبطل جميع سعيهم، واضمحل أكثر شبهاتهم، ولم يحصل لهم من القول بتقدمها على زمان وجود الفعل - مع أنها في الحال المتقدمة على وجود الفعل ليست بقدرة لهذا الفعل - إلا القول ببقاء الأعراض وهو باطل، والقول بوجود قدرة لا مقدور لها ولا مقارناً لها ولا متأخرا عنها، وهو محال.
[Is it] power for this act that accompanied it at the time of continuance? Or was it power not for this act but for another act that is done by it in the second [moment] from the time of the existence of this act? If they say: It is in the state of continuance power for another act other than this one that existed in the state of its continuance, and the power of this act is in the first of the states of its existence and the act follows it, then it is in the second, in its state of existence, power for another act that exists in the third of its states of existence—and that is the saying of some of them. We say: This is like the first; for since it was not in every state power for what accompanies it of the act, but rather it is power for what follows it of the act, every act is established not by its power, and the existence of what is power for something else does not bring any benefit and does not repel what we have obliged. And if they say: It is in the second power for what is established in it of the accompanying act. We say: If it is in the second power for what is established in it of the act, then it is the power of the act that accompanied it, and all their striving becomes void, and most of their doubts dissipate, and they obtain from the saying of its priority over the time of the existence of the act—while it is in the state prior to the existence of the act not being power for this act—nothing except the saying of the continuance of accidents, which is false, and the saying of the existence of a power that has no object of power, nor does it accompany it, nor is it posterior to it, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the relationship between divine power (qudra) and the act (fi'l) at the time of continuance (baqa'). The opponent suggests that when a power endures, it might be power for another act, not for the one that exists with it. Nasafi first restates the opponent's position: power in the state of continuance is for another act, while the power for the current act existed only in the first moment and the act followed it; then in the second moment, it becomes power for a third act. Nasafi responds that this is like the first objection, because if the power is never accompanying the act but always for a future act, then every act occurs without a power that is directly connected to it. This makes the act established without its power, which is unacceptable. He then considers another possibility: that in the second moment, power is for the act that occurs in that same moment. In that case, the power must be for the act it accompanies, which defeats their entire argument. Their efforts collapse, and their position leads to the false doctrine of the continuance of accidents (baqa' al-a'rad) and the absurdity of a power with no object of power (maqdur) at all.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
بقاء (baqa')
مقدور (maqdur)
أعراض (a'rad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can be temporally disconnected from its act; it can exist before or after the act without accompanying it.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if power is not directly accompanying its act, the act would have no power, which is impossible. Power and act must coincide.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Nasafi draw if the opponent says that in the second moment, power is for the act that occurs in that moment?
A) That power must be prior to the act.
B) That power then truly accompanies the act, and the opponent's position collapses.
C) That power is impossible.
D) That the act precedes power.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that any attempt to separate divine power from its act in time leads to logical impossibility; power must directly accompany its act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you attributed a success to your own effort without consciously recognizing God's direct power in that moment? How does Nasafi's argument challenge you to see your daily acts as directly accompanied by divine power?
?
If God's power must directly accompany every act, then nothing happens 'on its own' — including your sins and mistakes. Does this realization make you more humble or more defiant? Why?
?
This lesson refutes the idea that God's power can be disconnected from its effect. Are there areas of your life where you act as if God's power is distant or delayed, expecting results from secondary causes alone?

Can Power Exist Simultaneously with Its Act?
Nasafi questions whether the act can exist alongside power, leading to contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's dialectical argument that power must be able to coexist with its act, and the absurdity of the opposing view.

Core Idea
Power that cannot coexist with its act is actually impotence, not power.

This passage presents a classic kalam debate about whether divine power (qudra) can exist at the same time as its effect. Nasafi engages opponents who claim they cannot coexist. Through a series of questions, he exposes contradictions in their position, ultimately showing that if power did not accompany its act, it would be impotence and change would be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial question
Nasafi asks whether the act can exist simultaneously with power. Notice the two possible responses and their implications.
This sets up the dialectic.
2
Trace the first branch of argument
If they say 'no' (the act cannot coexist), they agree with Nasafi. If they say 'yes', the argument proceeds to the next question.
3
Analyze the change in power over time
Nasafi asks if power in the second moment is different from the first. If it changed, he argues that an accident cannot change by accepting a new meaning.
Key principle: accidents do not accept meanings that change them.
4
Examine the consequence of change
If power before the change was not power but impotence (since it couldn't produce the act), then the act must accompany it after change.
This leads to the conclusion that power can coexist with its act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إذا كانت القدرة في زمان وجودها موجودة، أيستحيل وجود الفعل معها أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا بما ذهب إليه خصومهم، وبطل جميع ما يتعلقون به من الشبه. وإن قالوا: نعم، قيل لهم: أليس أنها في الثاني من زمان وجودها غير ما كان في زمان وجودها؟ فلابد من بلى - قيل: وهل حدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت هي عليه في الأول أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد ارتكبوا محالاً، لما أن العرض لا يقبل معنى يتغير به وقيل لهم وهل كانت موجبة ثبوت الفعل بدون ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب تغيرها؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فلم يكن لوجود المعنى المغير أثر وكان وجوده كعدمه وإن قالوا: لا، قيل: فإذا كانت قبل ثبوت التغير لم تكن قدرة بل كانت عجزاً إذ ما يستحيل ثبوت الفعل به عجز لا قدرة، وإنما صار قدرة عند حدوث التغير في الثاني، وقد قارنها الفعل فصح ما قلنا وبطل سعيكم مع ما فيه من استحالة انقلاب ما هو في حكم العجز قدرة.
Then it is said to them: When power exists in the time of its existence, is the existence of the act possible with it or not? If they say: No, then they have affirmed what their opponents hold, and all the doubts they cling to become void. And if they say: Yes, it is said to them: Is it not that it is in the second of the time of its existence other than what it was in the time of its existence? It must be yes—it is said: And did there occur in it in the second a meaning that necessitated its change from what it was upon in the first or not? If they say: Yes, then they have committed an impossibility, because an accident does not accept a meaning by which it changes. And it is said to them: Was it necessitating the establishment of the act without that meaning which necessitated its change? If they say: Yes, then the existence of the changing meaning had no effect and its existence was like its non-existence. And if they say: No, it is said: Then if it was before the establishment of the change not power but rather impotence—since what makes the establishment of the act impossible by it is impotence, not power—and it only becomes power at the occurrence of the change in the second, and the act has accompanied it, so what we said is correct and your striving becomes void, along with the impossibility of what is in the ruling of impotence turning into power.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument. Nasafi first asks the opponent: When power exists, can the act exist simultaneously with it? If they say no (i.e., the act cannot exist with power), that would actually agree with Nasafi's position, because it implies that the act occurs without power directly accompanying it, which is what he argues. If they say yes (that the act can coexist), then Nasafi questions whether the power in the second moment is the same as in the first. If they say it changed (due to a new meaning), he points out that an accident (like power) cannot accept a new meaning—that would be impossible. Then he asks whether the power was necessitating the act before the change. If they say yes, then the change has no effect. If they say no, then before the change, the power was actually impotence ('ajz) because it could not bring about the act. Only after the change does it become power, but then the act accompanies it, which shows that power can exist simultaneously with its act—contradicting their earlier claim that it cannot. The opponent's position leads to the absurd conclusion that impotence turns into power.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
عجز ('ajz)
تغير (taghayyur)
معنى (ma'na)
عرض ('arad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that power cannot coexist with its act at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if power could not coexist with its act, then before the act, the power would be impotence, not power.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say about an accident (like power) changing by accepting a new meaning?
A) It is possible and common.
B) It is impossible because an accident does not accept a meaning by which it changes.
C) It is necessary for power to become effective.
D) It is a sign of divine omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the opponents' position leads to the absurd conclusion that impotence can become power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to have power over something—like your own actions or choices—do you ever stop to consider that without God's continuous creation, that power would instantly become impotence? How does this passage's logical dismantling of independent power challenge your usual sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Nasafi argues that if power could not coexist with its act, it would actually be impotence. Reflect on a time when you felt powerful but your intended action failed. What does that failure reveal about the nature of your own power—is it truly yours, or a borrowed capacity from Allah?
?
The opponent's attempt to separate power from its act leads to absurd contradictions. In your daily life, are there false dichotomies you hold—thinking, for example, that God's will and your responsibility are incompatible—that this argument might expose as equally untenable? What would it cost you to let go of that false dichotomy?

Refuting the Claim that Power Does Not Change
Nasafi refutes the claim that power remains unchanged while its relation to the act changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that if power were identical in two moments, its impossibility to attach to the act would persist, and how this refutes the opponent's claim.

Core Idea
Identical power cannot change its relation without a new meaning.

This lesson focuses on Nasafi's decisive refutation of the claim that power remains unchanged even as its relation to the act changes. The opponent argues that power does not acquire any new meaning in the second moment. Nasafi responds with a logical reductio: if power is the same, the impossibility of its attachment to the act must also be the same, leading to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the first line carefully. The opponent says no new meaning occurs in power in the second moment. Identify what 'first' and 'second' refer to (two moments of power's existence).
The opponent is likely a Mu'tazili.
2
Follow Nasafi's conditional argument
Nasafi says: if power in the first moment had impossible attachment to the act, then in the second moment, being the same, it must also have impossible attachment. Notice the logical structure: it's a conditional with an absurd consequence.
3
Recognize the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi extends the argument: if this were possible, one could also claim that impotence ('ajz) could be attached to an act in one state and impossible in another without change. Since that is impossible, the original claim is also impossible.
This shows the necessity of a changing meaning for a changing relation.
4
Reflect on the historical context
The passage ends with an anecdote about al-Sirafi being refuted in Shiraz. This shows the argument was used in actual debate. Consider how this argument would work in a live discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لم تحدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت في الأول. قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الأول موجودة ويستحيل تعلقها بالفعل وثبوته بها يستحيل ذلك أيضا في الثاني، لأنها عين ما كان يستحيل تعلقه بالفعل ويستحيل ثبوته به والقول بوجوب تعلق شيء بشيء ووجوب ثبوت الثاني بالأول أن ذلك كان لذاته مما يستحيل تعلقه بالثاني وثبوت الثاني بالأول قول محال، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز القول بوجوب تعلق العجز بالفعل ووجوب ثبوت الفعل به في حال وإن ووجوب استحال ذلك في حال من غیر حدوث معنی مغير ، وهذا عين المحال. وقد أورد هذا الفصل على السيرافي بشيراز فانقطع أفحش انقطاع.
If they say: No meaning occurred in it in the second that necessitated its change from what it was in the first. We say: If it was in the first existing and its attachment to the act is impossible and the act's establishment by it is impossible, that is also impossible in the second, because it is the same as what had impossible attachment to the act and impossible establishment of the act by it. And the statement of the necessity of attachment of something to something and the necessity of the establishment of the second by the first, when that [first] is by its very self such that attachment to the second is impossible and establishment of the second by the first is impossible, that statement is impossible. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible to say the necessity of attachment of impotence to the act and the necessity of the establishment of the act by it in one state, and the necessity of its impossibility in another state, without the occurrence of a changing meaning—and this is absolute impossibility. And this chapter was presented to al-Sirafi in Shiraz, and he was cut off most disgracefully.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage concludes the argument. The opponent may claim that power does not acquire any new meaning in the second moment; it remains the same as in the first. Nasafi responds: If in the first moment the power was such that attachment to the act was impossible (i.e., it could not bring about the act simultaneously), then since it is the same identical power in the second moment, it must still be impossible for it to attach to the act. To claim that the same thing can be impossible for an act in one state and possible in another without any change is absurd. If that were allowed, then one could also claim that impotence ('ajz) could be necessarily attached to an act in one state and necessarily impossible in another, without any change—which is clearly impossible. Nasafi mentions that this argument was presented to al-Sirafi (a Mu'tazili scholar) in Shiraz, and he was utterly refuted.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
عجز ('ajz)
تعلق (ta'allaq)
ثبوت (thubut)
محال (muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can have a different relation to the act in different moments without any change in the power itself.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if power is identical, then its impossibility or possibility with respect to the act must also be identical; otherwise, one could claim the same for impotence, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What follows if power is identical in both moments and its attachment to the act is impossible in the first?
A) It becomes possible in the second by itself.
B) It also remains impossible in the second.
C) It becomes necessary in the second.
D) It changes into a different kind of power.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the claim that power can change its relation without a new meaning by showing it would lead to the absurdity that impotence could also produce acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own ability to do good or avoid sin is fixed, without realizing that your heart's state must change for your actions to change? This lesson shows that identical power cannot produce different effects without a new meaning. What does this teach you about the need for constant renewal of your intention and reliance on Allah?
?
Nasafi's argument uses a reductio: if power could change its relation without change, then impotence could also produce acts. Reflect on a time you excused yourself from a duty by saying 'I just can't' — did you consider that your 'can't' might be a fixed state, and that without a change in you, it will remain? What change must you seek?
?
When you claim to be unable to do something for the sake of Allah, are you treating your power as if it were immutable, like the Mu'tazila claimed? This lesson forces you to confront: if you are capable of one action, but not another of the same kind, what has changed in you? Is it your will, your reliance, your certainty? Ask yourself right now: what one step can you take today to move from 'impossible' to 'possible' by seeking a new meaning in your heart?

Objection and Answer on Change in Power
The author refutes a shubha about change in qudra without new meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that divine power can change without an external cause, preserving the doctrine of God's unchangeable attributes.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot change; change would require an impossible accident.

This passage presents a detailed dialectical argument against the idea that God's power can change from one moment to another without a new meaning. The author systematically refutes the opponent's position by showing that such a change would lead to contradictions, such as converting incapacity into power or making an accident accept a meaning. This argument is crucial for understanding the Maturidi position on the immutability of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the dialectical style with questions and answers.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow rather than every word.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that power in the second moment is different from the first. Recognize this as the starting point of the debate.
The author uses 'shubha' meaning doubt or specious argument.
3
Trace the first branch: new meaning occurred
When the opponent says yes to a new meaning, the author argues this is impossible because an accident cannot accept a meaning that changes it. Then he asks whether the power was effective without that meaning, leading to further contradictions.
This branch shows the absurdity of positing a new meaning.
4
Trace the second branch: no new meaning
If the opponent says no new meaning, then the power before change was actually incapacity. The author argues that change from incapacity to power is impossible. Also, if no new meaning, the power is identical, so its relation to action remains impossible.
This branch shows the absurdity of denying any change.
5
Reflect on the historical conclusion
The passage ends with the author's argument being presented to al-Sirafi, who was completely confounded. This adds historical weight to the refutation.
Consider how such dialectical arguments were used in actual debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الشبه. وإن قالوا: نعم، قيل لهم: أليس أنها في الثاني من زمان وجودها غير ما كان في زمان وجودها؟ فلابد من بلى - قيل: وهل حدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت هي عليه في الأول أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد ارتكبوا محالاً، لما أن العرض لا يقبل معنى يتغير به وقيل لهم وهل كانت موجبة ثبوت الفعل بدون ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب تغيرها؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فلم يكن لوجود المعنى المغير أثر وكان وجوده كعدمه وإن قالوا: لا، قيل: فإذا كانت قبل ثبوت التغير لم تكن قدرة بل كانت عجزاً إذ ما يستحيل ثبوت الفعل به عجز لا قدرة، وإنما صار قدرة عند حدوث التغير في الثاني، وقد قارنها الفعل فصح ما قلنا وبطل سعيكم مع ما فيه من استحالة انقلاب ما هو في حكم العجز قدرة.
فإن قالوا: لم تحدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت في الأول.
مع
هي
عليه
قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الأول موجودة ويستحيل تعلقها بالفعل وثبوته بها يستحيل ذلك أيضا في الثاني، لأنها عين ما كان يستحيل تعلقه بالفعل ويستحيل ثبوته به والقول بوجوب تعلق شيء بشيء ووجوب ثبوت الثاني بالأول أن ذلك كان لذاته مما يستحيل تعلقه بالثاني وثبوت الثاني بالأول قول محال، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز القول بوجوب تعلق العجز بالفعل ووجوب ثبوت الفعل به في حال وإن ووجوب استحال ذلك في حال من غیر حدوث معنی مغير، وهذا عين المحال. وقد أورد هذا الفصل على السيرافي بشيراز فانقطع أفحش انقطاع
From the doubts. And if they say: Yes, it is said to them: Is it not that it in the second moment of its existence is other than what it was in the moment of its existence? Then inevitably yes - it is said: And did there occur in it in the second a meaning that necessitated its change from what it was upon in the first or not? If they say: Yes, then they have committed an impossibility, because an accident does not accept a meaning by which it changes; and it is said to them: Was it necessitating the establishment of the act without that meaning which necessitated its change? If they say: Yes, then the existence of the changing meaning had no effect and its existence was like its non-existence. And if they say: No, it is said: Then it before the establishment of the change was not power but was incapacity, since what makes the establishment of the act impossible is incapacity not power; and it only became power at the occurrence of the change in the second, and the act accompanied it, so what we said is correct and your endeavor is invalid along with what is in it of the impossibility of transformation of what is in the ruling of incapacity into power. If they say: There did not occur in it in the second a meaning that necessitated its change from what it was in the first. (with it upon) We say: If it in the first existed and it is impossible for its relation to the act and the act's being established by it, that is also impossible in the second, because it is the very thing whose relation to the act was impossible and the act's being established by it is impossible; and the statement that the relation of something to something and the establishment of the second by the first are necessary because that was due to its essence, which makes its relation to the second and the second's being established by the first impossible, is an impossible statement; and if that were permissible, then the statement that the relation of incapacity to the act and the establishment of the act by it is necessary in a state while its state being impossible in a state without the occurrence of a changing meaning would be permissible, and this is sheer impossibility. And this chapter was presented to al-Sirafi in Shiraz, and he was cut off most severely.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a doubt (shubha) raised by an opponent, likely a Mu'tazilite, regarding the nature of power (qudra) and its relation to action (fi'l). The author presents a dialectical exchange. The opponent claims that power in the second moment is different from the first. The author then asks whether a new meaning (ma'na) occurred in the second that caused the change. If yes, then since an accident cannot accept a meaning that changes it, that is impossible. If no, then the power before change was actually incapacity ('ajz), and the change from incapacity to power is impossible. The author further argues that if no new meaning occurred, then the power in the second is the same as in the first, and since relation to action was impossible in the first, it remains impossible. The argument concludes that such a change would necessitate that incapacity be related to action, which is absurd. The section ends with a historical note that this argument was presented to al-Sirafi in Shiraz, who was utterly confounded.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
قدرة (qudra)
عجز ('ajz)
عرض ('arad)
معنى (ma'na)
فعل (fi'l)
السيرافي (al-Sirafi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can change its nature without an external cause or new meaning.
Correction: Power is an accident and cannot accept a meaning that changes it; any change would turn power into incapacity or vice versa, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, if the opponent says no new meaning occurred in the second moment, what was the power in the first moment?
A) True power (qudra)
B) Incapacity ('ajz)
C) A potential power
D) A different type of power

Key Takeaway
Divine power is immutable; any change would entail impossible consequences such as an accident accepting a meaning or incapacity turning into power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's power is distant or diminished in your life? When you consider this lesson, is the issue a change in God or a change in your perception? How does the immutability of divine power confront that feeling?
?
If you truly believe that God's power cannot change, then when you face a difficulty you think is beyond you, do you doubt His ability to help you? How does the logical argument in this passage strengthen your reliance on Him?
?
Think of a time you assumed God's attribute of power was subject to change based on your circumstances. Now that you know change is impossible, what does that demand from you in terms of trust and patience? Will you repent from that assumption and realign your heart with this truth?

5 min
Argument from the Mu'tazila's Own Stance
Refutation using opponent's own premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila by using their own premises about power and action.

Core Idea
Using the opponent's premises to show their inconsistency.

This passage presents a clever argument where the author turns the Mu'tazila's own views against them. By examining their stance on when an act is valid and when an agent is described as having power, the author shows that their position leads to a contradiction: the agent can neither perform the act nor be called powerful. This sets the stage for the author's own proof on power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then the translation. Note the logical structure: 'if... then... so...'
The passage uses 'min madhhabikum' indicating it's drawing from the opponent's doctrine.
2
Identify the two premises
The Mu'tazila hold two premises: (1) in the first moment, the act is not valid from the capable agent; (2) in the second moment, the agent is not described as having power over the act.
3
See the conclusion drawn
The author combines both premises: in neither moment is the act possible from the agent, so the agent cannot be a doer or creator.
The conclusion is phrased rhetorically: 'how do you make him a doer?'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوحشه، وهو ثم نقول: من مذهبكم أن الفعل لا يصح من القادر في الأول، وفي الثاني لا يوصف هو بالقدرة على الفعل، فكان مجموع الحالين أنه لا يصح منه الفعل ولا يقدر عليه، فكيف تجعلونه فاعلاً بل خالقًا لقوله؟ وما لا يصح وجوده ولا قدرة عليه لا يتصور وجوده.
على أننا بينا من الكلام في جانب القدرة أن خلوها عن الفعل لو جاز وقتًا من مع وجود ذاتها لجاز أوقاتًا، إذ عرف بذلك أن ثبوت الفعل ليس من مقتضيات ذاتها لازم على هذا ويبطله ولا اعتراض لهم عليه.
And it made him more miserable, and he then says: From your madhhab (school of thought) is that the act is not valid from the capable (al-qadir) in the first, and in the second he is not described as having power over the act; so the sum of the two states is that the act is not valid from him and he has no power over it, so how do you make him a doer (fa'il) rather a creator of his speech? And that whose existence is not valid and over which there is no power is not conceivable to exist. Moreover, we have shown from the discussion concerning power (qudra) that its being empty of the act, if it were permissible at one time while its essence exists, would be permissible at multiple times, because it is known thereby that the establishment of the act is not a necessary requirement of its essence; this invalidates it and there is no objection against it.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments. First, the author argues from the Mu'tazila's own premises: they hold that in the first moment the act is not valid from the capable agent, and in the second moment the agent is not described as having power over the act. Therefore, in neither moment is the act possible from the agent, and the agent cannot be called a doer or creator. Second, the author presents his own proof: if power (qudra) could be free from the act at one time while its essence exists, then it could be free at multiple times, meaning that the act is not a necessary consequent of the essence of power. This argument invalidates the opponent's position and stands unobjectionable.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
قادر (qadir)
مذهب (madhhab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazila's position on power and action is internally consistent.
Correction: The author shows that by their own premises, the agent can never be considered powerful or the act possible, leading to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the author's argument from the Mu'tazila's own madhhab, what is the combined result of the two moments?
A) The act is valid in the first and power in the second
B) The act is not valid and there is no power over it
C) The agent is a doer in both moments
D) Power is present but action is absent

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own premises about power and action lead to the conclusion that the agent can never be considered powerful or the act possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that Allah is al-Qadir over all things. Yet consider: do you live as if some events are beyond His control? When anxiety strikes, do you forget that His power encompasses your ability to act? The Mu'tazila mistakenly separated power from action. Are there moments when your actions contradict your reliance on His power? What step will you take today to consistently affirm His qudra in your daily affairs?
?
The author uses the opponent's own premises to show inconsistency. Are there areas in your own belief where you hold contradictory premises? For example, do you affirm that Allah is all-powerful yet sometimes worry that outcomes are out of His control? How can you align your heart with the consistency of tawhid?
?
The passage ends by stating that what is impossible to exist and has no power over it cannot be conceived to exist. Think of a time you feared something that you knew was impossible for Allah? Did your heart still conceive it? How does this truth help you guard your thoughts and strengthen your tawakkul?

Author's Proof on Power and Action
Author's own proof about power and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that power (qudra) does not necessarily entail action, based on the permissibility of its being empty of action.

Core Idea
If power can be empty of action at one time, it can be at all times.

After refuting the Mu'tazila using their own premises, the author presents his own positive proof. He argues that if it is possible for power to be free from action at a single moment while its essence exists, then it would be possible for it to be free at multiple moments. This shows that action is not a necessary requirement of the essence of power, thereby invalidating the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The phrase 'min ma'a wujud dhatiha' is important.
2
Understand the conditional argument
The author says: if it were permissible for power to be empty of action at one time while its essence exists, then it would be permissible at multiple times. Why? Because the essence is the same at all times.
The key is that the essence of power does not change.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, the existence of action is not a necessary requirement of the essence of power. This refutes the opponent who might claim that power necessarily produces action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Or do you take it for granted, assuming it is automatic? Commit to reciting 'Alhamdulillah' with full presence of mind after every breath, acknowledging that each moment of existence is a fresh act of God's power.
?
The author argues that the presence of action is not a necessary requirement of power. How does this affect your view of God's mercy? Do you assume that God must forgive because of His mercy (attribute), or do you realize that mercy is also an act of will? If God's mercy is not necessary, then your repentance must be sincere and desperate. Ask yourself: have I been presumptuous about God's forgiveness? Today, repent with the utmost humility, recognizing that forgiveness is a free gift, not an obligation.

Refutation of Reward and Punishment Without Action
Refutation of the claim that reward/punishment need no action and defense of Sunni views on divine favor and children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the claim that reward and punishment do not require actions, and the justification of divine favor and compensation for children.

Core Idea
Recompense (jazā') is inherently tied to actions; divine favor (ifḍāl) transcends strict retribution.

This passage presents a key Sunni refutation against a sect that believed reward and punishment in the afterlife could occur without corresponding actions. The author argues from the Qur'an that recompense is always in exchange for deeds, and then defends the Sunni position on God's absolute sovereignty, including His right to bestow favor without prior merit and to allow children's pain with compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's innovation
Read the passage and note the opponent's claim: reward in Paradise and punishment in Hell that are not recompense for actions, but rather for refraining from prohibited acts or omitting commanded acts.
This is a rejection of the necessary connection between action and recompense.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The author cites Qur'an 56:24 to affirm that recompense (jazā') is in exchange for what people used to do. Understand how this verse refutes the opponent's position.
The verse is a direct evidence that reward is for actions.
3
Understand the Sunni position on divine favor and children
The opponent accuses Ahl al-Sunna of disbelief for claiming that God can bestow favor without prior merit and may inflict pain on children with compensation. Recognize that Sunnis affirm God's absolute sovereignty and wisdom, and that compensation ensures justice in the case of children.
This underscores the Sunni emphasis on divine grace (ifḍāl) over strict retribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أن الرجوع للحق وجعل القدرة مقارنة للفعل أولى من ارتكاب هذه المناكير التى يصير قائلها عبرة للعالمين في الحماقة والسخافة ومستحقا للخلود في النيران. ثم إنه لأجل هذه البدعة زعم أن في الجنة ثوابًا كثيرًا ليس بجزاء، وأن في النار عقابًا كثيرًا ليس بجزاء، ولأنه ينال الثواب لا على فعل بل لأنه لم يفعل المنهي عنه، ويستحق العقاب لا على فعل بل لأنه لم يفعل المأمور به، والجزاء ما يكون بمقابلة العمل على ما قال الله تعالى: جَزَاء بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ وعن غيرها من الآيات، ثم إن هذا رجل يكفر أهل السنة والجماعة وينسبهم إلى الضلال والبدعة بقولهم: إن الله تعالى ليس بمحجور عن الإفضال إلى عباده، وهو إعطاء ما لم يستحقوا عليه بأعمالهم، وبقولهم: إن الله تعالى إن يؤلم الأطفال من غیر استحقاق بفعل قبيح واشتراط عوض وإن كان ذلك الإفضال بشيء قليل لا
There is no doubt that returning to truth and making capability concomitant with action is better than committing these enormities which cause their speaker to become a lesson for the worlds in foolishness and stupidity, and deserving eternal abode in the fires. Then, because of this innovation, he claimed that in Paradise there is much reward that is not recompense, and in Hell much punishment that is not recompense. And because he obtains reward not for an action but because he did not do the forbidden thing, and he deserves punishment not for an action but because he did not do the commanded thing. But recompense is that which is in exchange for action, as God Almighty said: 'A recompense for what they used to do' (Surah al-Waqi'ah, 56:24) and other verses. Then this man declares the People of the Sunnah and the Community as disbelievers and attributes to them misguidance and innovation for their statement that God Almighty is not restricted from bestowing favor upon His servants, and that He gives what they do not deserve by their actions, and for their statement that God Almighty may inflict pain upon children without deserving [it] due to a bad act, and stipulating compensation, even if that bestowal is a small thing...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the opponent's position. The author first affirms that the correct doctrine—that capability (qudra) is concurrent with the act—is superior to the opponent's erroneous views. He then identifies a specific innovation (bid'a) of the opponent: the claim that there is reward in Paradise and punishment in Hell that are not recompense (jazā') for actions. The opponent holds that reward can be obtained without an action, simply by refraining from a prohibited act, and punishment can be incurred without an action, simply by omitting a commanded act. The author refutes this by citing the Qur'anic verse that equates recompense with what people have done. Furthermore, the author criticizes the opponent for declaring Ahl al-Sunna (Sunni Muslims) to be disbelievers and innovators because they hold that God is not prevented from bestowing favor (ifḍāl) on His servants without prior merit, and that God can inflict pain on children without them having committed any evil deed, while stipulating compensation. The argument underscores the Sunni affirmation of divine sovereignty and grace against a rationalist insistence on exact retribution.

ثواب
عقاب
إفضال
أطفال
عوض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that Ahl al-Sunna believe in unjustified punishment without compensation.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm God's absolute sovereignty and justice; when children experience pain, compensation is provided, rendering it just.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the opponent claim about reward in Paradise?
A) It is always a recompense for deeds.
B) It is given without any action, as a result of not doing the prohibited.
C) It is only for those who perform many good deeds.
D) It is determined by divine decree alone.

Key Takeaway
Recompense (jazā') is tied to actions, but God's favor (ifḍāl) is His to bestow, and He may allow children's pain with compensation, upholding His justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Independence Argument: Ability Must Be Simultaneous
Al-Nasafi presents the first proof: preceding ability leads to independence from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school argues that ability must be simultaneous with the act, and why preceding ability would imply the servant's independence from God, which is considered kufr.

Core Idea
If ability came before the act, the servant would not need God at the moment of action.

This lesson presents a key proof from the Maturidi theologian al-Nasafi. He argues that if a person's ability to act existed before the act itself, then at the very moment of the act, that person would not rely on God. Such independence from God, even for an instant, is a grave error. You will see how this logical argument grounds the simultaneous view of ability and act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the stark conclusion: believing ability precedes the act leads to kufr.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the logical consequence
The passage states that if ability precedes, then at the time of the act the servant is independent of Allah. Trace this reasoning: ability before act → servant not needing God for the act → independence.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on the gravity
Why is this considered kufr? Reflect on what it means to deny need for Allah at any moment. Consider how this connects to tawhid.
This argument shows how a seemingly technical point has massive theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقدم القدرة على الفعل يؤدي إلى الاستغناء عن الله]: ومن مشاهير الدلائل لنا في المسألة: أن القول بتقدم القدرة على الفعل يوجب استفتاء العهد عن الله تعالى وقت الفعل والقول بغنية العبد عن الله تعالى في لحظة من عمره كفر. وكذا إجماع أهل القبلة على سؤال المعونة وطلب التوفيق من الله تعالى في كل وقت، ولو كان الإنسان أعطى قدرة الطاعة قبل وجودها لم يكن للطلب معنى. وراء ذلك طرق كثيرة أوردها أئمتنا (۱) لوحة ٣٤٩ ظ د. (۲) ز و تابع أبو منصور – رحمه الله – في تقريرها ودفع الأسئلة عنها، واعتمد على أن إعطاء القدرة قبل الفعل خروج عن الحكمة، ولا يليق ذلك بحكمة الباري عز وجل، وبالغ فيه مبالغة عظيمة استقصى في تحقيقها (۱) ودفع الأسئلة عنها أعرضنا عن (ذكر ذلك كله) (۲) مخافة التطويل (۳)، والله الموفق.
The precedence of ability over the act leads to independence from Allah]: Among the famous proofs for us in this issue is that the saying that ability precedes the act necessitates the cancellation of the covenant (istifta' al-'ahd) with Allah Almighty at the time of the act, and saying that the servant is independent of Allah Almighty at any moment of his life is kufr. Similarly, the consensus (ijma') of Ahl al-Qibla on asking for help (su'al al-ma'una) and seeking divine facilitation (tawfiq) from Allah Almighty at every time, and if a person had been given the power of obedience before its existence, the request would have no meaning. Beyond that, there are many ways that our imams have mentioned... and our master Imam Abu Mansur – may Allah have mercy on him – was very thorough in their elucidation and in refuting the objections against them, and he relied on the fact that giving ability before the act is a departure from wisdom (khuruj 'an al-hikma), and that is not befitting the wisdom of the Creator, exalted and glorified. He greatly emphasized this, pursued its verification and the refutation of objections, but we have refrained from mentioning all of that for fear of prolixity. And Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the Maturidi/Ash'ari position that the ability (istita'a) to perform an act is simultaneous with the act, not preceding it. The author presents two main arguments: first, if ability preceded the act, then at the moment of the act the servant would be independent of God, which is kufr. Second, the consensus of the Muslim community on seeking God's help and tawfiq at every moment would be meaningless if the ability to obey were already present. The author mentions that many other proofs have been given by the imams, especially Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who argued that giving ability before the act is against divine wisdom. However, the author refrains from detailing them to avoid length.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
الفعل (al-fi'l)
الاستغناء (al-istighna')
كفر (kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of believing that ability precedes the act?
A) It makes the act impossible.
B) It implies the servant is independent of God at the time of the act.
C) It means God is not all-powerful.
D) It is a minor sin.

Key Takeaway
Believing that ability precedes the act leads to the conclusion that the servant is independent of God at the moment of action, which is kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which moments of your daily life do you unconsciously act as if your abilities are your own, forgetting that you depend on Allah for each action? How will you remind yourself of this truth?
?
When you succeed in something, do you silently attribute it to your own skill or preparation? How does this lesson challenge that tendency, and what can you say instead to acknowledge Allah's continuous grant of ability?
?
If you truly internalized that at every moment you need Allah's power to act, would you fear failing or would you rely on Him more? What is one area where you can start practicing this reliance today?

The Consensus and Wisdom Arguments for Simultaneity
Al-Nasafi presents the consensus on seeking help and the wisdom argument for simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional proofs: the consensus of Muslims on seeking God's help at every moment, and the argument from divine wisdom against giving ability before the act.

Core Idea
The universal practice of asking God's help proves ability cannot be pre-given; giving it beforehand contradicts divine wisdom.

This lesson continues al-Nasafi's argumentation with two further proofs. First, the consensus (ijma) of the Muslim community: they constantly ask God for tawfiq and ma'una, which would be pointless if ability were already present. Second, a more philosophical argument from Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: giving ability before the act would be against divine wisdom (hikma). The author, however, refrains from elaborating to avoid prolixity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage noting the two distinct arguments: the consensus argument and the wisdom argument. Observe how the author concludes by mentioning further proofs but not detailing them.
The footnote markers (1), (2), (3) indicate omitted material.
2
Analyze the consensus argument
Why does the constant asking for help imply that ability cannot precede? If the ability to obey were already given, there would be no need to ask. The fact that Muslims always ask shows they need God's continuous support.
This is a strong practical argument from the Sunna.
3
Understand the wisdom argument
Imam al-Maturidi argued that giving ability before the act is contrary to divine wisdom. Why? Because it would make God's ongoing involvement unnecessary, which is not befitting His perfect wisdom. Reflect on how this ties into God's continuous creation and sustenance of actions.
This argument is more philosophical and nuanced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that once God gives a person the ability to obey, they no longer need to ask for help.
Correction: The consensus on constant asking shows that ability is given moment by moment; we always depend on God.

Checkpoint
What does the consensus on asking for help imply about the timing of ability?
A) Ability precedes the act.
B) Ability is simultaneous with the act.
C) Ability comes after the act.
D) Ability is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The constant asking for divine help by Muslims proves that ability is not pre-given but is granted at the moment of action; divine wisdom also precludes giving ability beforehand.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Iyyaka na'budu wa iyyaka nasta'in' (You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help), do you truly feel that your ability to worship is being granted by Allah at that very moment, or do you assume you already have it? How does this lesson challenge your assumption?
?
Think of a time you tried to do something good but failed. Did you consider that perhaps you were relying on an ability you thought you already possessed, rather than asking Allah for it anew? What will you change in your mindset before your next act of obedience?
?
Abu Mansur argued that giving ability before the act would be a departure from divine wisdom. If you truly believed that your every good action is only possible because Allah grants ability at the moment, how would it change your humility, your reliance, and your gratitude? Will you start each act with an explicit request for that ability?
